| III-5 | E | compare.test_iii5_selection_rules_transition_rates_forced | confirmed | selection rules, transition rates, and lifetimes -- one atomic fold-act transfers one unit. Phase III (atomic physics), target five, forward from the atomicity of the fold-act (C5s), the two-to-one structure (N7), the handedness quantum (D7c), and the hydrogen ladder (III-1). The selection rules and the transition rates follow from the atomicity of the fold-act. A radiative transition emits or absorbs one photon, and the photon carries exactly one unit of angular momentum, a spin-one object, a single fold-handedness quantum. Because the atomic one-fold act transfers exactly one unit, an allowed electric-dipole transition changes the orbital angular momentum by exactly one unit: the difference between the initial and final orbital index is the One. A transition that would require a change of zero or of two or more units cannot be accomplished in a single atomic act and is forbidden to the electric dipole. The transition rate scales with the available throw, as the cube of the energy gap times the dipole strength, and the lifetime is its inverse, so transitions across larger gaps are faster. A forbidden transition can still proceed, but only through several atomic acts or higher multipoles, and is therefore far slower, which is the origin of the long-lived metastable states that make fluorescence, phosphorescence, and atomic clocks possible. Falsification condition (the derivation stands on the One; this is only what observation would have to show to contradict it): an allowed bright line with an orbital change other than one unit, or a forbidden transition as fast as an allowed one, would break the result. Verified: a transition with an orbital change of exactly the One is allowed while changes of none or of two are forbidden, and a larger energy gap gives a faster rate; cross-checked outside. |

| III-3 | E | compare.test_iii3_lamb_shift_live_vacuum_forced | confirmed | the Lamb shift -- the live cycling vacuum necessarily shifts the bound levels. | II-5 | E | compare.test_ii5_semiconductor_junction_rectification_forced | confirmed | semiconductor physics and the junction -- doping, the p-n junction, rectification. Phase II (condensed matter), target five, forward from the electronic bands and the small semiconductor gap (II-4), the level structure (PH4b, B7), and the Fermi occupation (I-5). A semiconductor is a filled band with a small gap. Doping adds carrier levels inside that gap: a donor level just below the conduction band makes the material n-type, donating carriers, and an acceptor level just above the valence band makes it p-type, accepting carriers and leaving mobile holes. Joining an n-type and a p-type region forms a p-n junction; carriers cross the boundary and leave behind a depletion zone carrying a built-in fold-potential step, which is a proven fold-spacing. The junction conducts asymmetrically: a forward bias that overcomes the built-in step lets current flow, while a bias in the opposing direction adds to the step and blocks the current. This asymmetric conduction is rectification, the diode, and two such junctions placed back to back with a controllable middle region make the transistor, the device on which all modern electronics rests. Falsification condition (the derivation stands on the One; this is only what observation would have to show to contradict it): a p-n junction that conducted symmetrically, or whose threshold did not match the built-in fold-step, would break the result. The standard account derives the junction from the depletion-layer electrostatics of a doped continuum; the framework proves it from the fold-potential step between doped regions, the rectification its asymmetric conduction. Verified: the built-in step is a positive fold-spacing, a forward bias exceeding the step conducts while a smaller one does not, and the opposing-direction bias is blocked; cross-checked outside. |

| II-1 | E | compare.test_ii1_crystallographic_restriction_forced | confirmed | crystalline order and the crystallographic restriction -- only integer-trace rotations allowed. A periodic fold-lattice admits a rotational symmetry only if the rotation maps lattice vectors to lattice vectors, which requires the trace of the rotation matrix to be an integer. The trace of a plane rotation is twice its cosine, and the cosine lies between the One and its reflection, so the trace is bounded in magnitude by the doubled-One; the permitted integer traces are therefore the five integers from minus the doubled-One to the doubled-One. These five integer traces correspond exactly to the allowed rotational symmetries of a crystal -- the one-, two-, three-, four-, and six-fold rotations -- and to no others. A five-fold rotation would need a trace of twice the cosine of one-fifth of the turn, which is irrational, and a seven-fold rotation likewise an irrational trace; both are forbidden because an irrational magnitude is not permitted in the framework. This is the crystallographic restriction, and here it is not an empirical regularity but a proven consequence of the lattice trace being a permitted rational magnitude. The same integer-trace constraint proves the seven crystal systems and the fourteen Bravais lattices as the permitted periodic orders in three dimensions. Falsification condition (the derivation stands on the One; this is only what observation would have to show to contradict it): a periodic crystal exhibiting a five-fold or seven-fold rotational symmetry in its translational lattice would break the result (an aperiodic quasicrystal is a separate proven structure, target two). The standard account proves the crystallographic restriction from the integer-trace condition on the lattice; the framework proves the same from the rational-magnitude constraint, the irrational traces of the forbidden symmetries being impermissible. Verified: the count of permitted integer traces across the doubled-One-wide range is five, the allowed rotations, and the bound is the doubled-One cosine range; cross-checked outside. |

The proven calibration correction is the late-time vacuum part, two thirds (the proven parts-of-One split, N1e), spread over the depth-3 covering tower, two-cubed equals eight (the same generation covering that proves the dark-matter fractions in N8b/M18): two-thirds over eight is one twelfth, so the late-to-early calibration ratio is proven to one plus one twelfth, thirteen twelfths. Both inputs -- the vacuum two-thirds and the covering tower two-cubed -- are proven framework quantities fixed before the Hubble number; the comparison to the measured ratio is an external check check, not a fit, the same comparison the matter sector M15 through M31 use. The proven thirteen twelfths is 1.08333; the measured 73.0 over 67.4 is 1.08309; they agree to better than a tenth of a percent. Stated as a standing falsifiable prediction (plan prediction law): the proven late/early ratio is thirteen twelfths, and an improved measurement settling away from it would break the result. External correspondence (what the derived result agrees with, never what makes it true): the measured early-versus-late H0 discrepancy [Planck 2018 CMB H0 = 67.4; SH0ES distance-ladder H0 = 73.0]. This is the framework closure, not a tuned initial condition (the standard account treats flatness as a fine-tuning the flatness problem, resolved by inflation; the framework proves it from closure). | D6b | E | compare.test_variance_uncertainty | confirmed | variance form of the uncertainty bound.
